</div>
			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Child development</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div role="note" class="hatnote">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Child_development_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Child development (disambiguation)">Child development (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Walter_de_Maria_Vertikaler_Erdkilometer.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Walter_de_Maria_Vertikaler_Erdkilometer.jpg/220px-Walter_de_Maria_Vertikaler_Erdkilometer.jpg" width="220" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Walter_de_Maria_Vertikaler_Erdkilometer.jpg/330px-Walter_de_Maria_Vertikaler_Erdkilometer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Walter_de_Maria_Vertikaler_Erdkilometer.jpg/440px-Walter_de_Maria_Vertikaler_Erdkilometer.jpg 2x" data-file-width="674" data-file-height="599" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Walter_de_Maria_Vertikaler_Erdkilometer.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Young child playing in <a href="/wiki/Squatting_position" title="Squatting position">squatting position</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Child development</b> entails the <a href="/wiki/Human_development_(biology)" title="Human development (biology)">biological</a>, <a href="/wiki/Developmental_psychology" title="Developmental psychology">psychological</a> and emotional changes that occur in human beings between birth and the end of <a href="/wiki/Adolescence" title="Adolescence">adolescence</a>, as the individual progresses from dependency to increasing <a href="/wiki/Autonomy" title="Autonomy">autonomy</a>. It is a continuous process with a predictable sequence yet having a unique course for every child. It does not progress at the same rate and each stage is affected by the preceding types of development. Because these developmental changes may be strongly influenced by genetic factors and events during prenatal life, genetics and prenatal development are usually included as part of the study of child development. Related terms include <a href="/wiki/Developmental_psychology" title="Developmental psychology">developmental psychology</a>, referring to development throughout the lifespan, and <a href="/wiki/Pediatrics" title="Pediatrics">pediatrics</a>, the branch of medicine relating to the care of children. Developmental change may occur as a result of genetically-controlled processes known as <a href="/wiki/Developmental_biology" title="Developmental biology">maturation</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Toga_2006_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Toga_2006-1">[1]</a></sup> or as a result of environmental factors and learning, but most commonly involves an interaction between the two. It may also occur as a result of human nature and our ability to learn from our environment.</p>
<p>There are various definitions of periods in a child's development, since each period is a continuum with individual differences regarding start and ending. Some age-related development periods and examples of defined intervals are: <a href="/wiki/Newborn" class="mw-redirect" title="Newborn">newborn</a> (ages 0–4 weeks); <a href="/wiki/Infant" title="Infant">infant</a> (ages 4 weeks – 1 year); <a href="/wiki/Toddler" title="Toddler">toddler</a> (ages 1–3 years); <a href="/wiki/Preschooler" class="mw-redirect" title="Preschooler">preschooler</a> (ages 4–6 years); <a href="/wiki/Elementary_school" title="Elementary school">school-aged child</a> (ages 6–13 years); <a href="/wiki/Adolescent" class="mw-redirect" title="Adolescent">adolescent</a> (ages 13–19).<sup id="cite_ref-kail_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kail-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Promoting child development through parental training, among other factors, promotes excellent rates of child development.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Parents play a large role in a child's life, socialization, and development. Having multiple parents can add stability to the child's life and therefore encourage healthy development.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Another influential factor in a child's development is the quality of their care. <a href="/wiki/Child_care" title="Child care">Child care</a> programs present a critical opportunity for the promotion of child development.</p>
<p>The optimal development of children is considered vital to society and so it is important to understand the social, cognitive, emotional, and educational development of children. Increased research and interest in this field has resulted in new theories and strategies, with specific regard to practice that promotes development within the school system. In addition there are also some theories that seek to describe a sequence of states that compose child development.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theories">Theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecological_systems">Ecological systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Ecological systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ecological_systems_theory" title="Ecological systems theory">Ecological systems theory</a></div>
<p>Also called "development in context" or "human ecology" theory, ecological systems theory, originally formulated by <a href="/wiki/Urie_Bronfenbrenner" title="Urie Bronfenbrenner">Urie Bronfenbrenner</a> specifies four types of nested environmental systems, with bi-directional influences within and between the systems. The four systems are microsystem, mesosystem, exosystem, and macrosystem. Each system contains roles, norms and rules that can powerfully shape development. Since its publication in 1979, Bronfenbrenner's major statement of this theory, <i>The Ecology of Human Development</i><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> has had widespread influence on the way psychologists and others approach the study of human beings and their environments. As a result of this influential conceptualization of development, these environments — from the family to economic and political structures — have come to be viewed as part of the life course from childhood through adulthood.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith.2C_Cowie.2C_Blades.2C_2011_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith.2C_Cowie.2C_Blades.2C_2011-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Piaget">Piaget</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Piaget">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Jean_Piaget" title="Jean Piaget">Jean Piaget</a> and <a href="/wiki/Piaget%27s_theory_of_cognitive_development" title="Piaget's theory of cognitive development">Piaget's theory of cognitive development</a></div>
<p>Jean Piaget was a Swiss scholar who began his studies in intellectual development in the 1920s. Piaget’s first interests were those that dealt with the ways in which animals adapt to their environments and his first scientific article about this subject was published when he was 10 years old. This eventually led him to pursue a Ph.D. in Zoology, which then led him to his second interest in epistemology.<sup id="cite_ref-SHAFFER_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SHAFFER-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">Epistemology</a> branches off from philosophy and deals with the origin of knowledge. Piaget believed the origin of knowledge came from Psychology, so he traveled to Paris and began working on the first “standardized intelligence test” at Alfred Binet laboratories; this influenced his career greatly. As he carried out this intelligence testing he began developing a profound interest in the way children’s intellectualism works. As a result, he developed his own laboratory and spent years recording children’s intellectual growth and attempted to find out how children develop through various stages of thinking. This led to Piaget develop four important stages of cognitive development: sensorimotor stage (birth to age 2), preoperational stage (age 2 to 7), concrete-operational stage (ages 7 to 12), and formal-operational stage (ages 11 to 12, and thereafter).<sup id="cite_ref-SHAFFER_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SHAFFER-7">[7]</a></sup> Piaget concluded that adaption to an environment (behaviour) is mananged through schemes and adaption occurs through <i>assimilation</i> and <i>accommodation</i>.<a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://www.newriver.edu/images/stories/library/Stennett_Psychology_Articles/Piagets%20Theory%20of%20Cognitive%20Development.pdf">[1]</a></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stages">Stages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Stages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b>Sensorimotor:</b> (birth to about age 2)</p>
<p>This is the first stage in Piaget's theory, where infants have the following basic senses: vision, hearing, and motor skills. In this stage, knowledge of the world is limited but is constantly developing due to the child's experiences and interactions.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> According to Piaget, when an infant reaches about 7–9 months of age they begin to develop what he called <a href="/wiki/Object_permanence" title="Object permanence">object permanence</a>, this means the child now has the ability to understand that objects keep existing even when they cannot be seen. An example of this would be hiding the child’s favorite toy under a blanket, although the child cannot physically see it they still know to look under the blanket.</p>
<p><b>Preoperational:</b> (begins about the time the child starts to talk about age 7)</p>
<p>During this stage of development, young children begin analyzing their environment using mental symbols. These symbols often include words and images and the child will begin to apply these various symbols in their everyday lives as they come across different objects, events, and situations.<sup id="cite_ref-SHAFFER_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SHAFFER-7">[7]</a></sup> However, Piaget’s main focus on this stage and the reason why he named it “preoperational” is because children at this point are not able to apply specific cognitive operations, such as mental math. In addition to symbolism, children start to engage in pretend play in which they pretend to be people they are not (teachers, superheroes). In addition, they sometimes use different props to make this pretend play more real.<sup id="cite_ref-SHAFFER_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SHAFFER-7">[7]</a></sup> Some deficiencies in this stage of development are that children who are about 3–4 years old often display what is called <a href="/wiki/Egocentrism" title="Egocentrism">egocentrism</a>, which means the child is not able to see someone else’s point of view, they feel as if every other person is experiencing the same events and feelings that they are experiencing. However, at about at 7 thought processes of children are no longer egocentric and are more intuitive, meaning they now think about the way something looks instead of rational thinking.<sup id="cite_ref-SHAFFER_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SHAFFER-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>Concrete:</b> (about first grade to early adolescence)</p>
<p>During this stage, children between the age of 7 and 11 use appropriate logic to develop cognitive operations and begin applying this new thinking to different events they may encounter.<sup id="cite_ref-SHAFFER_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SHAFFER-7">[7]</a></sup> Children in this stage incorporate <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">inductive reasoning</a>, which involves drawing conclusions from other observations in order to make a generalization.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Unlike the preoperational stage, children can now change and rearrange mental images and symbols to form a logical thought, an example of this is reversibility in which the child now has the ability to reverse an action just by doing the opposite.<sup id="cite_ref-SHAFFER_7-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SHAFFER-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>Formal operations:</b> (about early adolescence to mid/late adolescence)</p>
<p>The final stage of Piaget’s cognitive development defines a child as now having the ability to “think more rationally and systematically about abstract concepts and hypothetical events”.<sup id="cite_ref-SHAFFER_7-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SHAFFER-7">[7]</a></sup> Some positive aspects during this time is that child or adolescent begins forming their identity and begin understanding why people behave the way they behave. However, there are also some negative aspects which include the child or adolescent developing some egocentric thoughts which include the <a href="/wiki/Imaginary_audience" title="Imaginary audience">imaginary audience</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Personal_fable" title="Personal fable">personal fable</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SHAFFER_7-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SHAFFER-7">[7]</a></sup> An imaginary audience is when an adolescent feels that the world is just as concerned and judgemental of anything the adolescent does as they are, an adolescent may feel as is they are “on stage” and everyone is a critique and they are the ones being critiqued.<sup id="cite_ref-SHAFFER_7-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SHAFFER-7">[7]</a></sup> A personal fable is when the adolescent feels that he or she is a unique person and everything they do is unique. They feel as if they are the only ones that have ever experienced what they are experiencing and that they are invincible and nothing bad will happen to them it will only happen to others.<sup id="cite_ref-SHAFFER_7-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SHAFFER-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vygotsky">Vygotsky</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Vygotsky">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Lev_Vygotsky" title="Lev Vygotsky">Lev Vygotsky</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cultural-historical_psychology" title="Cultural-historical psychology">Cultural-historical psychology</a></div>
<p>Vygotsky was a Russian theorist, who proposed the sociocultural theory. During the 1920s–1930s while Piaget was developing his own theory, Vygotsky was an active scholar and at that time his theory was said to be “recent” because it was translated out of Russian language and began influencing Western thinking.<sup id="cite_ref-SHAFFER_7-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SHAFFER-7">[7]</a></sup> He posited that children learn through hands-on experience, as Piaget suggested. However, unlike Piaget, he claimed that timely and sensitive intervention by adults when a child is on the edge of learning a new task (called the <a href="/wiki/Zone_of_proximal_development" title="Zone of proximal development">zone of proximal development</a>) could help children learn new tasks. This technique is called "scaffolding," because it builds upon knowledge children already have with new knowledge that adults can help the child learn.<sup id="cite_ref-Vygotsky.2C_1930.3B_Cole.2C_1978_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vygotsky.2C_1930.3B_Cole.2C_1978-10">[10]</a></sup> An example of this might be when a parent "helps" an infant clap or roll her hands to the <a href="/wiki/Pat-a-cake" class="mw-redirect" title="Pat-a-cake">pat-a-cake</a> rhyme, until she can clap and roll her hands herself.<sup id="cite_ref-Vygotsky_1962_Expanded_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vygotsky_1962_Expanded-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wertsch.2C_1985_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wertsch.2C_1985-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Vygotsky was strongly focused on the role of culture in determining the child's pattern of development.<sup id="cite_ref-Vygotsky.2C_1930.3B_Cole.2C_1978_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vygotsky.2C_1930.3B_Cole.2C_1978-10">[10]</a></sup> He argued that "Every function in the child's cultural development appears twice: first, on the social level, and later, on the individual level; first, between people (interpsychological) and then inside the child (intrapsychological). This applies equally to voluntary attention, to logical memory, and to the formation of concepts. All the higher functions originate as actual relationships between individuals."<sup id="cite_ref-Vygotsky.2C_1930.3B_Cole.2C_1978_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vygotsky.2C_1930.3B_Cole.2C_1978-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Vygotsky felt that development was a process and saw periods of crisis in child development during which there was a qualitative transformation in the child's mental functioning.<sup id="cite_ref-Vygotsky.2C_1998_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vygotsky.2C_1998-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attachment">Attachment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Attachment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Attachment_theory" title="Attachment theory">Attachment theory</a></div>
<p>Attachment theory, originating in the work of <a href="/wiki/John_Bowlby" title="John Bowlby">John Bowlby</a> and developed by <a href="/wiki/Mary_Ainsworth" title="Mary Ainsworth">Mary Ainsworth</a>, is a <a href="/wiki/Psychological" class="mw-redirect" title="Psychological">psychological</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethology" title="Ethology">ethological</a> theory that provides a descriptive and explanatory framework for understanding <a href="/wiki/Interpersonal_relationship" title="Interpersonal relationship">interpersonal relationships</a> between human beings. Bowlby’s observations of close attachments led him to believe that close emotional bonds or “attachments” between an infant and their primary caregiver is an important requirement that is necessary to form “normal social and emotional development”.<sup id="cite_ref-SHAFFER_7-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SHAFFER-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Erik_Erikson">Erik Erikson</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Erik Erikson">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Erik_Erikson" title="Erik Erikson">Erik Erikson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psychosocial_development" class="mw-redirect" title="Psychosocial development">Psychosocial development</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Erik_Erikson" title="Erik Erikson">Erikson</a>, a follower of Freud's, synthesized both Freud's and his own theories to create what is known as the <a href="/wiki/Psychosocial_development" class="mw-redirect" title="Psychosocial development">"psychosocial"</a> stages of human development, which span from birth to death, and focuses on "tasks" at each stage that must be accomplished to successfully navigate life's challenges.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood.2C_Wood.2C_Boyd.2C_2010_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood.2C_Wood.2C_Boyd.2C_2010-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>Erikson's eight stages consist of the following:<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Trust vs. mistrust (infant)</li>
<li>Autonomy vs. shame (toddlerhood)</li>
<li>Initiative vs. guilt (preschooler)</li>
<li>Industry vs. inferiority (young adolescent)</li>
<li>Identity vs. role confusion (adolescent)</li>
<li>Intimacy vs. isolation (young adulthood)</li>
<li>Generativity vs. stagnation (middle adulthood)</li>
<li>Ego integrity vs. despair (old age)</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavioral">Behavioral</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Behavioral">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Behavior_analysis_of_child_development" title="Behavior analysis of child development">Behavior analysis of child development</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_B._Watson" title="John B. Watson">John B. Watson</a>’s <a href="/wiki/Behaviorism" title="Behaviorism">behaviorism</a> theory forms the foundation of the <a href="/wiki/Child_development_in_behavior_analytic_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Child development in behavior analytic theory">behavioral model of development</a> 1925.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Watson was able to explain the aspects of human psychology through the process of <a href="/wiki/Classical_conditioning" title="Classical conditioning">classical conditioning</a>. With this process, Watson believed that all individual differences in behavior were due to different learning experiences.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> He wrote extensively on child development and conducted research (see <a href="/wiki/Little_Albert_experiment" title="Little Albert experiment">Little Albert experiment</a>). This experiment had shown that phobia could be created by classical conditioning. Watson was instrumental in the modification of <a href="/wiki/William_James" title="William James">William James</a>’ stream of consciousness approach to construct a stream of <a href="/wiki/Learning_theory_(education)" title="Learning theory (education)">behavior theory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Watson also helped bring a natural science perspective to child psychology by introducing objective research methods based on observable and measurable behavior.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Following Watson’s lead, <a href="/wiki/B.F._Skinner" class="mw-redirect" title="B.F. Skinner">B.F. Skinner</a> further extended this model to cover <a href="/wiki/Operant_conditioning" title="Operant conditioning">operant conditioning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Verbal_behavior" class="mw-redirect" title="Verbal behavior">verbal behavior</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Skinner used the <a href="/wiki/Operant_chamber" class="mw-redirect" title="Operant chamber">operant chamber</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Skinner_box" class="mw-redirect" title="Skinner box">Skinner box</a>, to observe the behavior of small organisms in a controlled situation and proved that organisms' behaviors are influenced by the environment. Furthermore, he used reinforcement and punishment to shape in desired behavior.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In accordance with his view that the sexual drive is a basic human motivation,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a> developed a <a href="/wiki/Psychosexual_development" title="Psychosexual development">psychosexual theory of human development</a> from infancy onward, divided into five stages.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Each stage centered around the gratification of the libido within a particular area, or erogenous zone, of the body.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> He also argued that as humans develop, they become fixated on different and specific objects through their stages of development.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Each stage contains conflict which requires resolution to enable the child to develop.<sup id="cite_ref-Lemma.2C_2007_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lemma.2C_2007-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>The use of <a href="/wiki/Dynamical_systems_theory" title="Dynamical systems theory">dynamical systems theory</a> as a framework for the consideration of development began in the early 1990s and has continued into the present century.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Dynamic systems theory stresses nonlinear connections (e.g., between earlier and later social assertiveness) and the capacity of a system to reorganize as a phase shift that is stage-like in nature. Another useful concept for developmentalists is the attractor state, a condition (such as teething or stranger anxiety) that helps to determine apparently unrelated behaviors as well as related ones.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Dynamic systems theory has been applied extensively to the study of motor development; the theory also has strong associations with some of Bowlby's views about attachment systems. Dynamic systems theory also relates to the concept of the transactional process,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> a mutually interactive process in which children and parents simultaneously influence each other, producing developmental change in both over time.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The "core knowledge perspective" is an evolutionary theory in child development that proposes "infants begin life with innate, special-purpose knowledge systems referred to as core domains of thought"<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> There are five core domains of thought, each of which is crucial for survival, which simultaneously prepare us to develop key aspects of early cognition; they are: physical, numerical, linguistic, psychological, and biological.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Continuity_and_discontinuity">Continuity and discontinuity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Continuity and discontinuity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Although the identification of developmental milestones is of interest to researchers and to children's caregivers, many aspects of developmental change are continuous and do not display noticeable milestones of change.<sup id="cite_ref-Patterson.2C_2008_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patterson.2C_2008-23">[23]</a></sup> Continuous developmental changes, like growth in stature, involve fairly gradual and predictable progress toward adult characteristics. When developmental change is discontinuous, however, researchers may identify not only milestones of development, but related age periods often called stages. A stage is a period of time, often associated with a known chronological age range, during which a behavior or physical characteristic is qualitatively different from what it is at other ages. When an age period is referred to as a stage, the term implies not only this qualitative difference, but also a predictable sequence of developmental events, such that each stage is both preceded and followed by specific other periods associated with characteristic behavioral or physical qualities.<sup id="cite_ref-gross_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gross-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Stages of development may overlap or be associated with specific other aspects of development, such as speech or movement. Even within a particular developmental area, transition into a stage may not mean that the previous stage is completely finished. For example, in Erikson's discussion of stages of personality, this theorist suggests that a lifetime is spent in reworking issues that were originally characteristic of a childhood stage.<sup id="cite_ref-Erikson.2C_1968_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Erikson.2C_1968-25">[25]</a></sup> Similarly, the theorist of cognitive development, <a href="/wiki/Piaget%27s_theory_of_cognitive_development" title="Piaget's theory of cognitive development">Piaget</a>, described situations in which children could solve one type of problem using mature thinking skills, but could not accomplish this for less familiar problems, a phenomenon he called horizontal decalage.<sup id="cite_ref-mercer_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mercer-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanisms">Mechanisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Mechanisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
