</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nature_versus_nurture" title="Nature versus nurture">Nature versus nurture</a></div>
<p>Although developmental change runs parallel with chronological age,<sup id="cite_ref-feldman_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feldman-27">[27]</a></sup> age itself cannot cause development.<sup id="cite_ref-feldman_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feldman-27">[27]</a></sup> The basic mechanisms or causes of developmental change are genetic factors and environmental factors.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Genetic factors are responsible for cellular changes like overall growth, changes in proportion of body and brain parts,<sup id="cite_ref-Tau_2010_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tau_2010-29">[29]</a></sup> and the maturation of aspects of function such as vision and dietary needs.<sup id="cite_ref-feldman_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feldman-27">[27]</a></sup> Because genes can be "turned off" and "turned on",<sup id="cite_ref-feldman_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feldman-27">[27]</a></sup> the individual's initial genotype may change in function over time, giving rise to further developmental change. Environmental factors affecting development may include both diet and disease exposure, as well as social, emotional, and cognitive experiences.<sup id="cite_ref-feldman_27-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feldman-27">[27]</a></sup> However, examination of environmental factors also shows that young human beings can survive within a fairly broad range of environmental experiences.<sup id="cite_ref-mercer_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mercer-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rather than acting as independent mechanisms, genetic and environmental factors often interact to cause developmental change.<sup id="cite_ref-feldman_27-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feldman-27">[27]</a></sup> Some aspects of child development are notable for their <a href="/wiki/Neuroplasticity" title="Neuroplasticity">plasticity</a>, or the extent to which the direction of development is guided by environmental factors as well as initiated by genetic factors.<sup id="cite_ref-feldman_27-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feldman-27">[27]</a></sup> When an aspect of development is strongly affected by early experience, it is said to show a high degree of <a href="/wiki/Neuroplasticity" title="Neuroplasticity">plasticity</a>; when the genetic make-up is the primary cause of development, plasticity is said to be low.<sup id="cite_ref-buchwald_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buchwald-30">[30]</a></sup> Plasticity may involve guidance by endogenous factors like hormones as well as by exogenous factors like infection.<sup id="cite_ref-feldman_27-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feldman-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Soapbubbles-SteveEF.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Soapbubbles-SteveEF.jpg/220px-Soapbubbles-SteveEF.jpg" width="220" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Soapbubbles-SteveEF.jpg/330px-Soapbubbles-SteveEF.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Soapbubbles-SteveEF.jpg/440px-Soapbubbles-SteveEF.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1234" data-file-height="1049" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Soapbubbles-SteveEF.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Child playing with bubbles</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>One kind of environmental guidance of development has been described as experience-dependent plasticity, in which behavior is altered as a result of learning from the environment. Plasticity of this type can occur throughout the lifespan and may involve many kinds of behavior, including some emotional reactions.<sup id="cite_ref-feldman_27-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feldman-27">[27]</a></sup> A second type of plasticity, experience-expectant plasticity, involves the strong effect of specific experiences during limited sensitive periods of development.<sup id="cite_ref-feldman_27-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feldman-27">[27]</a></sup> For example, the coordinated use of the two eyes, and the experience of a single three-dimensional image rather than the two-dimensional images created by light in each eye, depend on experiences with vision during the second half of the first year of life.<sup id="cite_ref-feldman_27-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feldman-27">[27]</a></sup> Experience-expectant plasticity works to fine-tune aspects of development that cannot proceed to optimum outcomes as a result of genetic factors working alone.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenoug.2C_1987_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenoug.2C_1987-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnson.2C_1993_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson.2C_1993-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to the existence of plasticity in some aspects of development, genetic-environmental correlations may function in several ways to determine the mature characteristics of the individual. Genetic-environmental correlations are circumstances in which genetic factors make certain experiences more likely to occur.<sup id="cite_ref-feldman_27-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feldman-27">[27]</a></sup> For example, in passive genetic-environmental correlation, a child is likely to experience a particular environment because his or her parents' genetic make-up makes them likely to choose or create such an environment.<sup id="cite_ref-feldman_27-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feldman-27">[27]</a></sup> In evocative genetic-environmental correlation, the child's genetically-caused characteristics cause other people to respond in certain ways, providing a different environment than might occur for a genetically-different child;<sup id="cite_ref-feldman_27-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feldman-27">[27]</a></sup> for instance, a child with <a href="/wiki/Down_syndrome" title="Down syndrome">Down syndrome</a> may be treated more protectively and less challengingly than a non-Down child.<sup id="cite_ref-feldman_27-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feldman-27">[27]</a></sup> Finally, an active genetic-environmental correlation is one in which the child chooses experiences that in turn have their effect;<sup id="cite_ref-feldman_27-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feldman-27">[27]</a></sup> for instance, a muscular, active child may choose after-school sports experiences that create increased athletic skills, but perhaps preclude music lessons. In all of these cases, it becomes difficult to know whether child characteristics were shaped by genetic factors, by experiences, or by a combination of the two.<sup id="cite_ref-berk_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-berk-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_issues_and_methods">Research issues and methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Research issues and methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ol>
<li>What develops? What relevant aspects of the individual change over a period of time?</li>
<li>What are the rate and speed of development?</li>
<li>What are the mechanisms of development – what aspects of experience and heredity cause developmental change?</li>
<li>Are there typical individual differences in the relevant developmental changes?</li>
<li>Are there population differences in this aspect of development (for example, differences in the development of boys and of girls)?</li>
</ol>
<p>Empirical research that attempts to answer these questions may follow a number of patterns. Initially, observational research in naturalistic conditions may be needed to develop a narrative describing and defining an aspect of developmental change, such as changes in reflex reactions in the first year.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> This type of work may be followed by correlational studies, collecting information about chronological age and some type of development such as vocabulary growth; correlational statistics can be used to state change. Such studies examine the characteristics of children at different ages.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> These methods may involve longitudinal studies, in which a group of children are re-examined on a number of occasions as they get older,or cross-sectional studies, in which groups of children of different ages are tested once and compared with each other, or there may be a combination of these approaches. Some child development studies examine the effects of experience or heredity by comparing characteristics of different groups of children in a necessarily non-randomized design.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Other studies can use randomized designs to compare outcomes for groups of children who receive different interventions or educational treatments.<sup id="cite_ref-mercer_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mercer-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Milestones">Milestones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Milestones">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Child_development_stages" title="Child development stages">Child development stages</a></div>
<p>Milestones are changes in specific physical and mental abilities (such as walking and understanding language) that mark the end of one developmental period and the beginning of another.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> For stage theories, milestones indicate a stage transition. Studies of the accomplishment of many developmental tasks have established typical chronological ages associated with developmental milestones. However, there is considerable variation in the achievement of milestones, even between children with developmental trajectories within the typical range. Some milestones are more variable than others; for example, receptive speech indicators do not show much variation among children with typical hearing, but expressive speech milestones can be quite variable.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>A common concern in child development is <a href="/wiki/Developmental_delay" class="mw-redirect" title="Developmental delay">developmental delay</a> involving a delay in an age-specific ability for important developmental milestones. Prevention of and early intervention in developmental delay are significant topics in the study of child development.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Developmental delays should be diagnosed by comparison with characteristic variability of a milestone, not with respect to average age at achievement. An example of a milestone would be eye-hand coordination, which includes a child's increasing ability to manipulate objects in a coordinated manner. Increased knowledge of age-specific milestones allows parents and others to keep track of <a href="/wiki/Age-appropriateness" class="mw-redirect" title="Age-appropriateness">appropriate development</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>There is a phenomenal growth or exponential increase of child development from the age of 4 to 15 years old especially during the age of 4 to 7 years old based on the Yamana chart <sup id="cite_ref-dcya.gov.ie_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dcya.gov.ie-35">[35]</a></sup>). The Heckman's chart shows that the highest return of investment in education is maximum during the early years (age 1 to 3 years old) and decreases to a plateau during the school aged years and adolescence.<sup id="cite_ref-dcya.gov.ie_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dcya.gov.ie-35">[35]</a></sup> There are various child development tables or charts e.g. the PILES table where PILES stands for Physical, Intellectual, Language, Emotional and Social development aspects.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aspects">Aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Child development is not a matter of a single topic, but progresses somewhat differently for different aspects of the individual. Here are descriptions of the development of a number of physical and mental characteristics.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_growth">Physical growth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Physical growth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><span id="growth"></span> Physical growth in <a href="/wiki/Human_height" title="Human height">stature</a> and weight occurs over the 15–20 years following birth, as the individual changes from the average weight of 3.5&#160;kg and length of 50&#160;cm at <a href="/wiki/Full_term" class="mw-redirect" title="Full term">full term</a> birth to full adult size. As stature and weight increase, the individual's <a href="/wiki/Proportionality_(mathematics)" title="Proportionality (mathematics)">proportions</a> also change, from the relatively large head and small <a href="/wiki/Torso" title="Torso">torso</a> and <a href="/wiki/Limb_(anatomy)" title="Limb (anatomy)">limbs</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Infant" title="Infant">neonate</a>, to the adult's relatively small head and long torso and limbs.<sup id="cite_ref-tanner_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tanner-37">[37]</a></sup> The child’s pattern of growth is in a head-to-toe direction, or cephalocaudal, and in an inward to outward pattern (center of the body to the peripheral) called proximodistal.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Speed_and_pattern">Speed and pattern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Speed and pattern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The speed of physical growth is rapid in the months after birth, then slows, so birth weight is doubled in the first four months, tripled by age 12 months, but not quadrupled until 24 months.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Growth then proceeds at a slow rate until shortly before puberty (between about 9 and 15 years of age), when a period of rapid growth occurs.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Growth is not uniform in rate and timing across all body parts. At birth, head size is already relatively near to that of an adult, but the lower parts of the body are much smaller than adult size. In the course of development, then, the head grows relatively little, and torso and limbs undergo a great deal of growth.<sup id="cite_ref-tanner_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tanner-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanisms_of_change">Mechanisms of change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Mechanisms of change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Genetic factors play a major role in determining the growth rate, and particularly the changes in proportion characteristic of early human development. However, genetic factors can produce the maximum growth only if environmental conditions are adequate. Poor nutrition and frequent injury and disease can reduce the individual's adult stature, but the best environment cannot cause growth to a greater stature than is determined by heredity.<sup id="cite_ref-tanner_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tanner-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Individual_variation_versus_disease">Individual variation versus disease</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Individual variation versus disease">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Individual differences in height and weight during childhood are considerable. Some of these differences are due to family genetic factors, others to environmental factors, but at some points in development they may be strongly influenced by individual differences in reproductive maturation.<sup id="cite_ref-tanner_37-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tanner-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Endocrinology#Professional_organizations" title="Endocrinology">American Association of Clinical Endocrinologists</a> defines <a href="/wiki/Short_stature" title="Short stature">short stature</a> as height more than 2 <a href="/wiki/Standard_deviation" title="Standard deviation">standard deviations</a> below the <a href="/wiki/Mean_(statistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mean (statistics)">mean</a> for age and gender, which corresponds to the shortest 2.3% of individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-urlFDA_Approves_Humatrope_for_Short_Stature_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-urlFDA_Approves_Humatrope_for_Short_Stature-38">[38]</a></sup> In contrast, <a href="/wiki/Failure_to_thrive" title="Failure to thrive">failure to thrive</a> is usually defined in terms of <a href="/wiki/Weight" title="Weight">weight</a>, and can be evaluated either by a low weight for the child's age, or by a low rate of increase in the weight.<sup id="cite_ref-urlFailure_to_Thrive:_Miscellaneous_Disorders_in_Infants_and_Children:_Merck_Manual_Professional_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-urlFailure_to_Thrive:_Miscellaneous_Disorders_in_Infants_and_Children:_Merck_Manual_Professional-39">[39]</a></sup> A similar term, <a href="/wiki/Stunted_growth" title="Stunted growth">stunted growth</a>, generally refers to reduced growth rate as a manifestation of <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a> in early childhood.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Motor">Motor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Motor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Early_toddler.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Early_toddler.jpg/220px-Early_toddler.jpg" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Early_toddler.jpg/330px-Early_toddler.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Early_toddler.jpg/440px-Early_toddler.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2576" data-file-height="1952" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Early_toddler.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A child learning to walk</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Abilities for physical movement change through childhood from the largely reflexive (unlearned, involuntary) movement patterns of the young infant to the highly skilled voluntary movements characteristic of later childhood and adolescence.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>"Motor learning refers to the increasing spatial and temporal accuracy of movements with practice".<sup id="cite_ref-Willingham_1999_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willingham_1999-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Motor_skills" class="mw-redirect" title="Motor skills">Motor skills</a> can be divided into two categories: first as basic skills necessary for everyday life and secondly, as recreational skills such as skills for employment or certain specialties based on interest.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Speed_and_pattern_2">Speed and pattern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Speed and pattern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The speed of motor development is rapid in early life, as many of the reflexes of the newborn alter or disappear within the first year, and slows later. Like physical growth, motor development shows predictable patterns of cephalocaudal (head to foot) and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Proximodistal&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Proximodistal (page does not exist)">proximodistal</a> (<a href="/wiki/Torso" title="Torso">torso</a> to extremities) development, with movements at the head and in the more central areas coming under control before those of the lower part of the body or the hands and feet. Types of movement develop in stage-like sequences;<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> for example, locomotion at 6–8 months involves creeping on all fours, then proceeds to pulling to stand, "cruising" while holding on to an object, <a href="/wiki/Walking" title="Walking">walking</a> while holding an adult's hand, and finally walking independently.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Older children continue the sequence by walking sideways or backward, galloping, hopping, skipping with one foot and walking with the other, and finally skipping.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> By middle childhood and adolescence, new motor skills are acquired by instruction or observation rather than in a predictable sequence.<sup id="cite_ref-Patterson.2C_2008_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patterson.2C_2008-23">[23]</a></sup> There are <a href="/wiki/Executive_functions" title="Executive functions">executive functions</a> of the brain (working memory, timing measure of inhibition and switching) which are important to motor skills. Critiques to the order of Executive Functioning leads to Motor Skills, suggesting Motor Skills can support Executive Functioning in the brain.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanisms_2">Mechanisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Mechanisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The mechanisms involved in motor development involve some genetic components that determine the physical size of body parts at a given age, as well as aspects of muscle and bone strength.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The main areas of the brain involved in motor skills are the <a href="/wiki/Frontal_cortex" class="mw-redirect" title="Frontal cortex">frontal cortex</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parietal_cortex" class="mw-redirect" title="Parietal cortex">parietal cortex</a> and <a href="/wiki/Basal_ganglia" title="Basal ganglia">basal ganglia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Prefrontal_cortex#Subdivisions" title="Prefrontal cortex">dorsolateral frontal cortex</a> is responsible for strategic processing. The parietal cortex is important in controlling perceptual-motor integration and the basal ganglia and supplementary <a href="/wiki/Motor_cortex" title="Motor cortex">motor cortex</a> are responsible for motor sequences. Nutrition and exercise also determine strength and therefore the ease and accuracy with which a body part can be moved.<sup id="cite_ref-Patterson.2C_2008_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patterson.2C_2008-23">[23]</a></sup> Flexibility is also affected by nutrition and exercise as well.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> It has also been shown that the <a href="/wiki/Frontal_lobe" title="Frontal lobe">frontal lobe</a> develops posterio-anteriorally (from back to front). This is significant in motor development because the hind portion of the frontal lobe is known to control motor functions. This form of development is known as "Portional Development" and explains why motor functions develop relatively quickly during typical childhood development, while logic, which is controlled by the middle and front portions of the frontal lobe, usually will not develop until late childhood and early adolescence.<sup id="cite_ref-Soska_2010_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soska_2010-42">[42]</a></sup> Opportunities to carry out movements help establish the abilities to flex (move toward the trunk) and extend body parts, both capacities are necessary for good motor ability. Skilled voluntary movements such as passing objects from hand to hand develop as a result of practice and learning.<sup id="cite_ref-Patterson.2C_2008_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patterson.2C_2008-23">[23]</a></sup> Mastery Climate is a suggested successful learning environment for children to promote motor skills by their own motivation. This promotes participation and active learning in children, which according to <a href="/wiki/Piaget%27s_theory_of_cognitive_development" title="Piaget's theory of cognitive development">Piaget's theory of cognitive development</a> is extremely important in early childhood rule.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Individual_differences">Individual differences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Individual differences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Typical individual differences in motor ability are common and depend in part on the child's weight and build. However, after the infant period, typical individual differences are strongly affected by opportunities to practice, observe, and be instructed on specific movements. Atypical motor development such as persistent <a href="/wiki/Primitive_reflex" class="mw-redirect" title="Primitive reflex">primitive reflexes</a> beyond 4–6 months or delayed walking may be an indication of <a href="/wiki/Developmental_delay" class="mw-redirect" title="Developmental delay">developmental delays</a> or conditions such as <a href="/wiki/Autism" title="Autism">autism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_palsy" title="Cerebral palsy">cerebral palsy</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Down_syndrome" title="Down syndrome">down syndrome</a> .<sup id="cite_ref-Patterson.2C_2008_23-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patterson.2C_2008-23">[23]</a></sup> Lower motor coordination results in difficulties with speed accuracy and trade-off in complex tasks.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Children_with_disabilities">Children with disabilities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Children with disabilities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Children with <a href="/wiki/Down_syndrome" title="Down syndrome">Down syndrome</a> or <a href="/wiki/Developmental_coordination_disorder" title="Developmental coordination disorder">Developmental coordination disorder</a> are late to reach major motor skills milestones. A few examples of these milestones are sucking, grasping, rolling, sitting up and walking, <a href="/wiki/Developmental_verbal_dyspraxia" title="Developmental verbal dyspraxia">talking</a>. Children with Down syndrome sometimes have heart problems, <a href="/wiki/Otitis_media" title="Otitis media">frequent ear infections</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypotonia" title="Hypotonia">hypotonia</a>, or undeveloped muscle mass. This <a href="/wiki/Syndrome" title="Syndrome">syndrome</a> is caused by atypical <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosomal</a> development. Along with Down syndrome, children can also be diagnosed with a learning disability. <a href="/wiki/Learning_disabilities" class="mw-redirect" title="Learning disabilities">Learning Disabilities</a> include disabilities in any of the areas related to language, reading, and mathematics. Basic reading skills is the most common learning disability in children, which, like other disabilities, focuses on the difference between a child’s academic achievement and his or her apparent capacity to learn.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_differences">Population differences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Population differences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Regardless of the culture a baby is born into, they are born with a few core domains of knowledge. These principals allow him or her to make sense of their environment and learn upon previous experience by using motor skills such as grasping or crawling. There are some population differences in motor development, with girls showing some advantages in small muscle usage, including <a href="/wiki/Articulation_(phonetics)" title="Articulation (phonetics)">articulation</a> of sounds with lips and tongue.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Ethnic differences in reflex movements of newborn infants have been reported, suggesting that some biological factor is at work.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Cultural differences may encourage learning of motor skills like using the left hand only for sanitary purposes and the right hand for all other uses, producing a population difference.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Cultural factors are also seen at work in practiced voluntary movements such as the use of the foot to dribble a soccer ball or the hand to dribble a basketball.<sup id="cite_ref-Patterson.2C_2008_23-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patterson.2C_2008-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cognitive.2Fintellectual">Cognitive/intellectual</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Cognitive/intellectual">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(June 2008)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cognitive_development" title="Cognitive development">Cognitive development</a> is primarily concerned with ways in which infants and children acquire, develop, and use internal mental capabilities such as <a href="/wiki/Problem_solving" title="Problem solving">problem solving</a>, <a href="/wiki/Working_memory" title="Working memory">memory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="What_develops.3F">What develops?</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: What develops?">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The capacity to <a href="/wiki/Learn" class="mw-redirect" title="Learn">learn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Recall_(memory)" title="Recall (memory)">remember</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Symbol" title="Symbol">symbolise</a> <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a>, and to solve <a href="/wiki/Problem" class="mw-redirect" title="Problem">problems</a>, exists at a simple level in young infants, who can perform cognitive tasks such as discriminating animate and inanimate beings or recognizing small numbers of objects.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> During childhood, learning and information-processing increase in speed, <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a> becomes increasingly longer, and symbol use and the capacity for <a href="/wiki/Abstraction" title="Abstraction">abstraction</a> develop, until a near-adult level is reached by <a href="/wiki/Adolescence" title="Adolescence">adolescence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Patterson.2C_2008_23-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patterson.2C_2008-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanisms_3">Mechanisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Mechanisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cognitive_development" title="Cognitive development">Cognitive development</a> has <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetic</a> and other biological mechanisms, as is seen in the many genetic causes of <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_disability" title="Intellectual disability">intellectual disability</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Environmental factors including food and <a href="/wiki/Nutrition" title="Nutrition">nutrition</a>, responsiveness of parents, daily experiences, physical activity and love can influence early brain development of children.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> However, although it is assumed that <a href="/wiki/Brain_function" class="mw-redirect" title="Brain function">brain functions</a> cause cognitive events, it has not been possible to measure specific brain changes and show that they cause cognitive change.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Developmental advances in <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognition</a> are also related to experience and learning, and this is particularly the case for higher-level abilities like abstraction, which depend to a considerable extent on formal education.<sup id="cite_ref-Patterson.2C_2008_23-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patterson.2C_2008-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Speed_and_pattern_3">Speed and pattern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Speed and pattern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The ability to learn temporal patterns in sequenced actions was investigated in elementary-school age children. <a href="/wiki/Temporal_logic" title="Temporal logic">Temporal learning</a> depends upon a process of integrating timing patterns with action sequences. Children ages 6–13 and young adults performed a serial response time task in which a response and a timing sequence were presented repeatedly in a phase-matched manner, allowing for <a href="/wiki/Multisensory_integration" title="Multisensory integration">integrative learning</a>. The degree of integrative learning was measured as the slowing in performance that resulted when phase-shifting the sequences. Learning was similar for the children and adults on average but increased with age for the children. Executive function measured by <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_Card_Sorting_Test" title="Wisconsin Card Sorting Test">Wisconsin Card Sorting Test (WCST)</a> performance as well as a measure of response speed also improved with age. Finally, WCST performance and response speed predicted temporal learning. Taken together, the results indicate that temporal learning continues to develop in pre-adolescents and that maturing executive function or processing speed may play an important role in acquiring temporal patterns in sequenced actions and the development of this ability.<sup id="cite_ref-Shin_2011_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shin_2011-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Individual_differences_2">Individual differences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Individual differences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There are typical individual differences in the ages at which specific cognitive abilities are achieved,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> but schooling for children in industrialized countries is based on the assumption that these differences are not large.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Atypical delays in cognitive development are problematic for children in cultures that demand advanced cognitive skills for work and for independent living.<sup id="cite_ref-Patterson.2C_2008_23-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patterson.2C_2008-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_differences_2">Population differences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Population differences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There are few population differences in cognitive development.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Boys and girls show some differences in their skills and preferences, but there is a great deal of overlap between the groups.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Differences in cognitive achievement of different ethnic groups appears to result from cultural or other environmental factors.<sup id="cite_ref-Patterson.2C_2008_23-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patterson.2C_2008-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social-emotional">Social-emotional</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Social-emotional">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Factors">Factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Newborn infants do not seem to experience fear or have preferences for contact with any specific people. In the first few months they only experience happiness, sadness, and anger.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> A baby’s first smile usually occurs between 6 and 10 weeks. It is called a ‘social smile’ because it usually occurs during social interactions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> By about 8–12 months, they go through a fairly rapid change and become fearful of <a href="/wiki/Perception" title="Perception">perceived threats</a>; they also begin to prefer familiar people and show anxiety and distress when separated from them or approached by strangers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Separation anxiety is a typical stage of development to an extent. Kicking, screaming, and throwing temper <a href="/wiki/Tantrum" title="Tantrum">tantrums</a> are perfectly typical symptoms for <a href="/wiki/Separation_anxiety" class="mw-redirect" title="Separation anxiety">separation anxiety</a>. Depending on the level of intensity, one may determine whether or not a child has <a href="/wiki/Separation_anxiety_disorder" title="Separation anxiety disorder">separation anxiety disorder</a>. This is when a child constantly refuses to separate from the parent, but in an intense manner. This can be given special treatment but the parent usually cannot do anything about the situation.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>The capacity for <a href="/wiki/Empathy" title="Empathy">empathy</a> and the understanding of social rules begin in the preschool period and continue to develop into adulthood.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Middle childhood is characterized by friendships with age-mates, and adolescence by emotions connected with sexuality and the beginnings of romantic love.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Anger seems most intense during the toddler and early preschool period and during adolescence.<sup id="cite_ref-Patterson.2C_2008_23-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patterson.2C_2008-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Speed_and_pattern_4">Speed and pattern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Speed and pattern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some aspects of social-emotional development,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> like empathy,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> develop gradually, but others, like fearfulness,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> seem to involve a rather sudden reorganization of the child's experience of emotion.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Sexual and romantic emotions develop in connection with physical maturation.<sup id="cite_ref-Patterson.2C_2008_23-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patterson.2C_2008-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanisms_4">Mechanisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Mechanisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Genetic factors appear to regulate some social-emotional developments that occur at predictable ages, such as fearfulness, and <a href="/wiki/Attachment_theory" title="Attachment theory">attachment</a> to familiar people. Experience plays a role in determining which people are familiar, which <a href="/wiki/Social_rules" class="mw-redirect" title="Social rules">social rules</a> are obeyed, and how anger is expressed.<sup id="cite_ref-Patterson.2C_2008_23-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patterson.2C_2008-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Parenting practices have been shown to predict children's emotional intelligence. The objective is to study the time mothers and children spent together in joint activity, the types of activities that they develop when they are together, and the relation that those activities have with the children's trait emotional intelligence. Data was collected for both mothers and children (N = 159) using self-report questionnaires. Correlations between time variables and trait emotional intelligence dimensions were computed using <a href="/wiki/Pearson_product-moment_correlation_coefficient" class="mw-redirect" title="Pearson product-moment correlation coefficient">Pearson's Product-Moment Correlation Coefficient</a>. Partial correlations between the same variables controlling for responsive parenting were also computed. The amount of time mothers spent with their children and the quality of their interactions are important in terms of children's trait emotional intelligence, not only because those times of joint activity reflect a more positive parenting, but because they are likely to promote modeling, reinforcement, shared attention, and social cooperation.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_differences_3">Population differences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Population differences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Population differences may occur in older children, if, for example they have learned that it is appropriate for boys to express emotion or behave differently from girls,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> or if customs learned by children of one ethnic group are different from those learned in another.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Social and emotional differences between boys and girls of a given age may also be associated with differences in the timing of <a href="/wiki/Puberty" title="Puberty">puberty</a> characteristic of the two sexes.<sup id="cite_ref-Patterson.2C_2008_23-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patterson.2C_2008-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language_and_communication">Language and communication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Language and communication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Language_development" title="Language development">Language development</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanisms_5">Mechanisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Mechanisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Language serves the purpose of communication to express oneself though a systematic and traditional use of sounds, signs, or written symbols.<sup id="cite_ref-hoff2014_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoff2014-48">[48]</a></sup> There are four subcomponents in which the child must attain in order to acquire language competence. They include phonology, lexicon, morphology and syntax, and pragmatics.<sup id="cite_ref-E_Learn_Portal_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-E_Learn_Portal-49">[49]</a></sup> These subcomponents of language development are combined to form the components of language, which are <a href="/wiki/Sociolinguistics" title="Sociolinguistics">sociolinguistics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Literacy" title="Literacy">literacy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hoff2014_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoff2014-48">[48]</a></sup> Currently, there is no single accepted theory of language acquisition but various explanations of language development have been accumulated.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Components">Components</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Components">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The four components of language development include:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">Phonology</a> is concerned with the sounds of language.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> It is the function, behavior, and organization of sounds as linguistic items.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Phonology considers what the sounds of language are and what the rules are for combining sounds. Phonological acquisition in children can be measured by accuracy and frequency of production of various vowels and consonants, the acquisition of phonemic contrasts and distinctive features, or by viewing development in regular stages in their own speech sound systems and to characterize systematic strategies they adopt.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lexicon" title="Lexicon">Lexicon</a> is a complex dictionary of words that enables language speakers to use these words in speech production and comprehension.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Lexicon is the inventory of a language's <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morphemes</a>. Morphemes act as minimal meaning-bearing elements or building blocks of something in language that makes sense. For example, in the word "cat", the component "cat" makes sense as does "at", but "at" does not mean the same thing as "cat". In this example, "ca" does not mean anything.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">Morphology</a> is the study of form or forms. It is the mental system involved in word formation or to the branch of linguistics that deals with words, their internal structure and how they are formed.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pragmatics" title="Pragmatics">Pragmatics</a> is the study of relationships between linguistic forms and the users of those forms.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> It also incorporates the use of utterance to serve different functions and can be defined as the ability to communicate one's feelings and desires to others.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Children’s development of language also includes <a href="/wiki/Semantics" title="Semantics">semantics</a> which is the attachment of meaning to words. This happens in three stages. First, each word means an entire sentence. For example, a young child may say “mama” but the child may mean “Here is Mama”, “Where is Mama?”, or “I see Mama.” In the second stage, words have meaning but do not have complete definitions. This stage occurs around age two or three. Third, around age seven or eight, words have adult-like definitions and their meanings are more complete.<sup id="cite_ref-hopper1973_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hopper1973-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>A child learns the <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">syntax</a> of his or her language when he or she is able to join words together into sentences and understand multiple-word sentences said by other people. There appear to be six major stages in which a child’s acquisition of syntax develops.<sup id="cite_ref-wood1976_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wood1976-58">[58]</a></sup> First, is the use of sentence-like words in which the child communicates using one word with additional vocal and bodily cues. This stage usually occurs between 12 and 18 months of age. Second, between 18 months to two years, there is the modification stage where children communicate relationships by modifying a topic word. The third stage, between two and three years old, involves the child using complete subject-predicate structures to communicate relationships. Fourth, children make changes on basic sentence structure that enables them to communicate more complex relationships. This stage occurs between the ages of two and a half years to four years. The fifth stage of categorization involves children aged three and a half to seven years refining their sentences with more purposeful word choice that reflects their complex system of categorizing word types. Finally, children use structures of language that involve more complicate syntactic relationships between the ages of five years old to ten years old.<sup id="cite_ref-wood1976_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wood1976-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Milestones_2">Milestones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Milestones">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Infants begin with cooing and soft vowel sounds. Shortly after birth, this system is developed as the infants begin to understand that their noises, or non-verbal communication, lead to a response from their caregiver.<sup id="cite_ref-Hart_and_Risley_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hart_and_Risley-59">[59]</a></sup> This will then progress into babbling around 5 months of age, with infants first babbling consonant and vowel sounds together that may sound like "ma" or "da".<sup id="cite_ref-woolfolk2012_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-woolfolk2012-60">[60]</a></sup> At around 8 months of age, babbling increases to include repetition of sounds, such as "da-da" and infants learn the forms for words and which sounds are more likely to follow other sounds.<sup id="cite_ref-woolfolk2012_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-woolfolk2012-60">[60]</a></sup> At this stage, much of the child’s communication is open to interpretation. For example, if a child says “bah” when they’re in a toy room with their guardian, it is likely to be interpreted as “ball” because the toy is in sight. However, if you were to listen to the same ‘word’ on a recorded tape without knowing the context, one might not be able to figure out what the child was trying to say.<sup id="cite_ref-Hart_and_Risley_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hart_and_Risley-59">[59]</a></sup> A child's <a href="/wiki/Receptive_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Receptive language">receptive language</a>, the understanding of others' <a href="/wiki/Speech" title="Speech">speech</a>, has a gradual development beginning at about 6 months.<sup id="cite_ref-pal2011_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pal2011-61">[61]</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Expressive_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Expressive language">expressive language</a>, the production of words, moves rapidly after its beginning at about a year of age, with a "vocabulary explosion" of rapid word acquisition occurring in the middle of the second year.<sup id="cite_ref-pal2011_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pal2011-61">[61]</a></sup> Grammatical rules and word combinations appear at about age two.<sup id="cite_ref-pal2011_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pal2011-61">[61]</a></sup> Between 20 and 28 months, children move from understanding the difference between high and low, hot and cold and begin to change “no” to “wait a minute”, “not now” and “why”. Eventually, they are able to add pronouns to words and combine them to form short sentences.<sup id="cite_ref-Hart_and_Risley_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hart_and_Risley-59">[59]</a></sup> Mastery of vocabulary and <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">grammar</a> continue gradually through the preschool and school years.<sup id="cite_ref-pal2011_61-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pal2011-61">[61]</a></sup> Adolescents still have smaller vocabularies than adults and experience more difficulty with constructions such as the passive voice.<sup id="cite_ref-pal2011_61-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pal2011-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>By age 1, the child is able to say 1–2 words, responds to its name, imitates familiar sounds and can follow simple instructions.<sup id="cite_ref-woolfolk2012_60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-woolfolk2012-60">[60]</a></sup> Between 1–2 years old, the child uses 5–20 words, is able to say 2-word sentences and is able to express their wishes by saying words like "more" or "up", and they understand the word "no".<sup id="cite_ref-woolfolk2012_60-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-woolfolk2012-60">[60]</a></sup> During 2 and 3 years of age, the child is able to refer to itself as "me", combine nouns and verbs, has a vocabulary of about 450 words, use short sentences, use some simple plurals and is able to answer "where" questions.<sup id="cite_ref-woolfolk2012_60-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-woolfolk2012-60">[60]</a></sup> By age 4, children are able to use sentences of 4–5 words and has a vocabulary of about 1000 words.<sup id="cite_ref-woolfolk2012_60-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-woolfolk2012-60">[60]</a></sup> Children between the ages of 4 and 5 years old are able to use past tense, have a vocabulary of about 1,500 words, and ask questions like "why?" and "who?".<sup id="cite_ref-woolfolk2012_60-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-woolfolk2012-60">[60]</a></sup> By age 6, the child has a vocabulary of 2,600 words, is able to form sentences of 5–6 words and use a variety of different types of sentences.<sup id="cite_ref-woolfolk2012_60-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-woolfolk2012-60">[60]</a></sup> By the age of 5 or 6 years old, the majority of children have mastered the basics of their native language.<sup id="cite_ref-woolfolk2012_60-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-woolfolk2012-60">[60]</a></sup> Infants, 15 month-olds, are initially unable to understand familiar words in their native language pronounced using an unfamiliar accent.<sup id="cite_ref-apa.org_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apa.org-62">[62]</a></sup> This means that a Canadian-English speaking infant cannot recognize familiar words pronounced with an Australian-English accent. This skill develops close to their second birthdays.<sup id="cite_ref-apa.org_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apa.org-62">[62]</a></sup> However, this can be overcome when a highly familiar story is read in the new accent prior to the test, suggesting the essential functions of underlying spoken language is in place before previously thought.<sup id="cite_ref-apa.org_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apa.org-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Vocabulary typically grows from about 20 words at 18 months to around 200 words at 21 months.<sup id="cite_ref-pal2011_61-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pal2011-61">[61]</a></sup> From around 18 months the child starts to combine words into two word sentences.<sup id="cite_ref-pal2011_61-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pal2011-61">[61]</a></sup> Typically the adult expands it to clarify meaning.<sup id="cite_ref-pal2011_61-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pal2011-61">[61]</a></sup> By 24–27 months the child is producing three or four word sentences using a logical, if not strictly correct, syntax.<sup id="cite_ref-pal2011_61-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pal2011-61">[61]</a></sup> The theory is that children apply a basic set of rules such as adding 's' for plurals or inventing simpler words out of words too complicated to repeat like "choskit" for <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_biscuit" title="Chocolate biscuit">chocolate biscuit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pal2011_61-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pal2011-61">[61]</a></sup> Following this there is a rapid appearance of grammatical rules and ordering of sentences.<sup id="cite_ref-pal2011_61-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pal2011-61">[61]</a></sup> There is often an interest in <a href="/wiki/Rhyme" title="Rhyme">rhyme</a>, and imaginative play frequently includes conversations. Children's recorded monologues give insight into the development of the process of organizing information into meaningful units.<sup id="cite_ref-pal2011_61-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pal2011-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>By three years the child begins to use complex sentences, including relative clauses, although still perfecting various <a href="/wiki/Linguistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Linguistic">linguistic</a> systems.<sup id="cite_ref-pal2011_61-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pal2011-61">[61]</a></sup> By five years of age the child's use of language is very similar to that of an adult.<sup id="cite_ref-pal2011_61-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pal2011-61">[61]</a></sup> From the age of about three children can indicate fantasy or make-believe linguistics, produce coherent personal stories and fictional narrative with beginnings and endings.<sup id="cite_ref-pal2011_61-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pal2011-61">[61]</a></sup> It is argued that children devise narrative as a way of understanding their own experience and as a medium for communicating their meaning to others.<sup id="cite_ref-pal2011_61-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pal2011-61">[61]</a></sup> The ability to engage in extended discourse emerges over time from regular conversation with adults and peers. For this the child needs to learn to combine his perspective with that of others and with outside events and learn to use linguistic indicators to show he is doing this. They also learn to adjust their language depending on to whom they are speaking.<sup id="cite_ref-pal2011_61-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pal2011-61">[61]</a></sup> Typically by the age of about 9 a child can recount other narratives in addition to their own experiences, from the perspectives of the author, the characters in the story and their own views.<sup id="cite_ref-pal2011_61-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pal2011-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sequential_skill_in_learning_to_talk">Sequential skill in learning to talk</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Sequential skill in learning to talk">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
