<p><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Theories_2">Theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Although the role of adult discourse is important in facilitating the child's learning, there is considerable disagreement among theorists about the extent to which children's early meanings and expressive words arise. Findings about the initial mapping of new words, the ability to decontextualize words, and refine meaning of words are diverse.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith.2C_Cowie.2C_Blades.2C_2011_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith.2C_Cowie.2C_Blades.2C_2011-6">[6]</a></sup> One hypothesis is known as the syntactic <a href="/wiki/Bootstrapping" title="Bootstrapping">bootstrapping</a> hypothesis which refers to the child's ability to infer meaning from cues, using grammatical information from the structure of sentences.<sup id="cite_ref-gleitman_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gleitman-64">[64]</a></sup> Another is the multi-route model in which it is argued that context-bound words and referential words follow different routes; the first being mapped onto event representations and the latter onto mental representations. In this model, parental input has a critical role but the children ultimately rely on cognitive processing to establish subsequent use of words.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrett_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrett-65">[65]</a></sup> However, naturalistic research on language development has indicated that preschoolers' vocabularies are strongly associated with the number of words addressed to them by adults.<sup id="cite_ref-hartris_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hartris-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is no single accepted theory of language acquisition. Instead, there are current theories that help to explain theories of language, theories of cognition, and theories of development. They include the generativist theory, <a href="/wiki/Social_interactionist_theory" title="Social interactionist theory">social interactionist theory</a>, usage-based theory (<a href="/wiki/Michael_Tomasello" title="Michael Tomasello">Tomasello</a>), connectionist theory, and behaviorist theory (<a href="/wiki/B._F._Skinner" title="B. F. Skinner">Skinner</a>). Generativist theories refer to Universal Grammar being innate where language experience activates innate knowledge.<sup id="cite_ref-hoff2009_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoff2009-67">[67]</a></sup> Social interactionist theories define language as a social phenomenon. This theory states that children acquire language because they want to communicate with others; this theory is heavily based on social-cognitive abilities that drive the language acquisition process.<sup id="cite_ref-hoff2009_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoff2009-67">[67]</a></sup> Usage-based theories define language as a set of formulas that emerge from the child’s learning abilities in correspondence with its social cognitive interpretation and understanding of the speakers’ intended meanings.<sup id="cite_ref-hoff2009_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoff2009-67">[67]</a></sup> Connectionist theories is a pattern-learning procedure and defines language as a system composed of smaller subsystems or patterns of sound or meaning.<sup id="cite_ref-hoff2009_67-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoff2009-67">[67]</a></sup> Behaviorist theories define language as the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Positive_reinforcement" class="mw-redirect" title="Positive reinforcement">positive reinforcement</a>, but is now regarded a theory of historical interest.<sup id="cite_ref-hoff2009_67-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoff2009-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Communication can be defined as the exchange and negotiation of information between two or more individuals through verbal and nonverbal symbols, oral and written (or visual) modes, and the production and comprehension processes of communication.<sup id="cite_ref-richards1986_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-richards1986-68">[68]</a></sup> According to First International Congress for the Study of Child Language, “the general hypothesis [is that] access to social interaction is a prerequisite to normal language acquisition”.<sup id="cite_ref-Dale_and_Snow_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dale_and_Snow-69">[69]</a></sup> Principles of conversation include two or more people focusing on one topic. All questions in a conversation should be answered, comments should be understood or acknowledged and any form of direction should, in theory, be followed. In the case of young, undeveloped children, these conversations are expected to be basic or redundant. The role of a guardians during developing stages is to convey that conversation is meant to have a purpose, as well as teaching them to recognize the other speaker’s emotions.<sup id="cite_ref-Dale_and_Snow_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dale_and_Snow-69">[69]</a></sup> Communicative language is nonverbal and/or verbal, and to achieve communication competence, four components must be met. These four components of communication competence include: grammatical competence (vocabulary knowledge, rules of word sentence formation, etc.), sociolinguistic competence (appropriateness of meanings and grammatical forms in different social contexts), discourse competence (knowledge required to combine forms and meanings), and strategic competence (knowledge of verbal and nonverbal communication strategies).<sup id="cite_ref-richards1986_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-richards1986-68">[68]</a></sup> The attainment of communicative competence is an essential part of actual communication.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Language development is viewed as a motive to communication, and the communicative function of language in-turn provides the motive for language development. Jean Piaget uses the term “acted conversations” to explain a child’s style of communication that rely more heavily on gestures and body movements, rather than words.<sup id="cite_ref-wood1976_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wood1976-58">[58]</a></sup> Younger children depend on gestures for a direct statement of their message. As they begin to acquire more language, body movements take on a different role and begin to complement the verbal message.<sup id="cite_ref-wood1976_58-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wood1976-58">[58]</a></sup> These nonverbal bodily movements allow children to express their emotions before they can express them verbally. The child’s nonverbal communication of how they’re feeling is seen in babies 0 to 3 months who use wild, jerky movements of the body to show excitement or distress.<sup id="cite_ref-wood1976_58-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wood1976-58">[58]</a></sup> This develops to more rhythmic movements of the entire body at 3 to 5 months to demonstrate the child’s anger or delight.<sup id="cite_ref-wood1976_58-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wood1976-58">[58]</a></sup> Between 9–12 months of age, children view themselves as joining the communicative world.<sup id="cite_ref-hoff2014_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoff2014-48">[48]</a></sup> Before 9–12 months, babies interact with objects and interact with people, but they do not interact with people about objects. This developmental change is the change from primary <a href="/wiki/Intersubjectivity" title="Intersubjectivity">intersubjectivity</a> (capacity to share oneself with others) to secondary intersubjectivity (capacity to share one’s experience), which changes the infant from an unsociable to socially engaging creature.<sup id="cite_ref-hoff2014_48-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoff2014-48">[48]</a></sup> Around 12 months of age a communicative use of gesture is used. This gesture includes communicative pointing where an infant points to request something, or to point to provide information.<sup id="cite_ref-hoff2014_48-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoff2014-48">[48]</a></sup> Another gesture of communication is presented around the age of 10 and 11 months where infants start gaze-following; they look where another person is looking.<sup id="cite_ref-hoff2014_48-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoff2014-48">[48]</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Joint_attention" title="Joint attention">joint attention</a> result in changes to their social cognitive skills between the ages of 9 and 15 months as their time is spent increasingly with others.<sup id="cite_ref-hoff2014_48-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoff2014-48">[48]</a></sup> Children’s use of non-verbal communicative gestures foretells future language development. The use of non-verbal communication in the form of gestures indicate the child’s interest in communication development, and the meanings they choose to convey that are soon revealed through the verbalization of language.<sup id="cite_ref-hoff2014_48-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoff2014-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Language acquisition and development contribute to the verbal form of communication. Children originate with a linguistic system where words they learn, are the words used for functional meaning.<sup id="cite_ref-hoff2009_67-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoff2009-67">[67]</a></sup> This instigation of speech has been termed pragmatic <a href="/wiki/Bootstrapping" title="Bootstrapping">bootstrapping</a>. According to this, children view words as a means of social construction, and that words are used to connect the understanding of communicative intentions of the speaker who speaks a new word.<sup id="cite_ref-hoff2009_67-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoff2009-67">[67]</a></sup> Hence, the competence of verbal communication through language is achieved through the attainability of <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">syntax</a> or <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">grammar</a>. Another function of communication through language is pragmatic development.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Pragmatic development includes the child’s intentions of communication before he/she knows how to express these intentions, and throughout the first few years of life both language and communicative functions develop.<sup id="cite_ref-hoff2009_67-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoff2009-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>When children acquire language and learn to use language for communicative functions (pragmatics), children also gain knowledge about the participation in conversations and relating to past experiences/events (discourse knowledge), and how to use language appropriately in congruence with their social situation or social group (sociolinguistic knowledge).<sup id="cite_ref-hoff2009_67-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoff2009-67">[67]</a></sup> Within the first two years of life, a child’s language ability progresses and conversational skills, such as the mechanics of verbal interaction, develop. Mechanics of verbal interaction include taking turns, initiating topics, repairing miscommunication, and responding to lengthen or sustain dialogue.<sup id="cite_ref-hoff2009_67-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoff2009-67">[67]</a></sup> Conversation is asymmetrical when a child interacts with an adult because the adult is the one to create structure in the conversation, and to build upon the child’s contributions. In accordance to the child’s developing conversational skills, asymmetrical conversation between adult and child modulate to an equal temperament of conversation. This shift in balance of conversation suggests a narrative discourse development in communication.<sup id="cite_ref-hoff2009_67-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoff2009-67">[67]</a></sup> Ordinarily, the development of communicative competence and the development of language are positively correlated with one another,<sup id="cite_ref-hoff2009_67-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoff2009-67">[67]</a></sup> however, the correlation is not flawless.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Individual_differences_3">Individual differences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Individual differences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Delays in language is the most frequent type of developmental delay. According to demographics 1 out of 5 children will learn to talk or use words later than other children their age. <a href="/wiki/Speech_delay" title="Speech delay">Speech</a>/<a href="/wiki/Language_delay" title="Language delay">language delay</a> is three to four times more common in boys than in girls. Some children will also display behavioral problems due to their frustration of not being able to express what they want or need.</p>
<p>Simple speech delays are usually temporary. Most cases are solved on their own or with a little extra attribution from the family. It’s the parent’s duty to encourage their baby to talk to them with gestures or sounds and for them to spend a great amount of time playing with, reading to, and communicating with their baby. In certain circumstances, parents will have to seek professional help, such as a <a href="/wiki/Speech-language_pathology" title="Speech-language pathology">speech therapist</a>.</p>
<p>It is important to take into considerations that sometimes delays can be a warning sign of more serious conditions that could include <a href="/wiki/Auditory_processing_disorder" title="Auditory processing disorder">auditory processing disorders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hearing_loss" title="Hearing loss">hearing loss</a>, <a href="/wiki/Developmental_verbal_dyspraxia" title="Developmental verbal dyspraxia">developmental verbal dyspraxia</a>, developmental delay in other areas, or even an autism spectrum disorder (ASD).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_causes">Environmental causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Environmental causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There are many environmental causes that are linked to language delays and they include situations such as, the child is having their full attention on other skills, such as walking perfectly, rather than on language. The child may have a twin or a sibling in which their age are relatively close, and may not be receiving the parent’s full attention. Another circumstance could be a child that is in a daycare that provides few adults to be able to administer individual attention. Perhaps the most obvious component would be a child that suffers from psychosocial deprivation such as poverty, malnutrition, poor housing, neglect, inadequate linguistic stimulation, or emotional stress.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Neurological_causes">Neurological causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Neurological causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Language delay can be caused by a substantial amount of underlying disorders, such as intellectual disability. <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_disability" title="Intellectual disability">Intellectual disability</a> takes part for more than 50 percent of language delays. Language delay is usually more rigorous than other developmental delays in intellectually disabled children, and it is usually the first obvious symptom of intellectual disability. Intellectual disability accounts to global language delay, including delayed auditory comprehension and use of gestures.</p>
<p>Impaired hearing is one of the most common causes of language delay. A child who can not hear or process speech in a clear and consistent manner will have a <a href="/wiki/Language_delay" title="Language delay">language delay</a>. Even the most minimum <a href="/wiki/Hearing_impairment" class="mw-redirect" title="Hearing impairment">hearing impairment</a> or <a href="/wiki/Auditory_processing_disorder" title="Auditory processing disorder">auditory processing deficit</a> can considerably affect language development. Essentially, the more the severe the impairment, the more serious the language delay. Nevertheless, <a href="/wiki/Deaf" class="mw-redirect" title="Deaf">deaf</a> children that are born to families who use <a href="/wiki/Sign_language" title="Sign language">sign language</a> develop infant babble and use a fully expressive sign language at the same pace as hearing children.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dyslexia" title="Dyslexia">Developmental Dyslexia</a> is a developmental reading disorder that occurs when the brain does not properly recognize and process the graphic symbols chosen by society to represent the sounds of speech. Children with dyslexia may encounter problems in rhyming and separating sounds that compose words. These abilities are essential in learning to read. Early <a href="/wiki/Reading_(process)" title="Reading (process)">reading</a> skills rely heavily on word recognition. When using an <a href="/wiki/Alphabet" title="Alphabet">alphabet</a> <a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">writing system</a> this involves in having the ability to separate out the sounds in words and be able to match them with letter and groups of letters. Because they have trouble in connecting sounds of language to the letter of words, this may result difficulty in understanding sentences. They have confusion in mistaking letters such as "b" and "d". For the most part, symptoms of dyslexia may include, difficulty in determining the meaning of a simple sentence, learning to recognize written words, and difficulty in rhyming.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Autism" title="Autism">Autism</a> and speech delay are usually correlated. Problems with verbal language are the most common signs seen in autism. Early diagnosis and treatment of autism can significantly help the child improve their speech skills. Autism is recognized as one of the five pervasive developmental disorders, distinguished by problems with language, speech, communication and social skills that present in early childhood. Some common autistic syndromes are the following, being limited to no verbal speech, <a href="/wiki/Echolalia" title="Echolalia">echolalia</a> or repeating words out of context, problems responding to verbal instruction and may ignore others who speak directly.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Risk_factors">Risk factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Risk factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Child development can be negatively influenced by a number of risk factors, many of which have been studied in developing countries.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> They can be broadly grouped under two headings.</p>
<ul>
<li>Hereditary</li>
<li>Environmental factors</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dt>1. Pre-natal environment</dt>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Factors related to mothers during pregnancy</dt>
<dd>
<ul>
<li class="mw-empty-elt"></li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd>
<ul>
<li>Nutritional deficiencies</li>
<li>Diabetic mother</li>
<li>Exposure to radiation</li>
<li>Infection with German measles</li>
<li>Smoking</li>
<li>Use of drugs</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>
<ul>
<li>Factors related to fetus</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd>
<ul>
<li>Mal-position in uterus</li>
<li>Faulty placental implantation</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>2. Post-Natal Environment</dt>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>
<ul>
<li>External environment</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd>
<ul>
<li>Socio-economic status of the family</li>
<li>Child’s nutrition</li>
<li>Exposure to radiation</li>
<li>Climate and season</li>
<li>Child’s ordinal position in the family</li>
<li>Number of siblings in the family</li>
<li>Family structure (single parent or extended family etc.)</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
</dd>

</ul>
</dd>

<dl>

<dl>

<li>Child’s intelligence</li>
<li>Hormonal influences</li>
<li>Emotions</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>Malnutrition, maternal depression and maternal substance abuse are three of these factors which have received particular attention by researchers, however, many more factors have been considered.<sup id="cite_ref-Mora_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mora-73">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kurstjens_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurstjens-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Frank_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frank-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Postnatal_depression">Postnatal depression</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Postnatal depression">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Postpartum_depression" title="Postpartum depression">Postpartum depression</a></div>
<p>Although there are a large number of studies contemplating the effect of maternal depression and postnatal depression of various areas of infant development, they are yet to come to consensus regarding the true effects. There are numerous studies indicating impaired development, and equally there are many proclaiming no effect of depression on development. A study of 18-month-olds whose mothers suffered depressive symptoms while they were 6 weeks and/or 6 months old indicated that maternal depression had no effect on the child’s cognitive development at 18 months.<sup id="cite_ref-Piteo_2012_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Piteo_2012-76">[76]</a></sup> Furthermore, the study indicates that maternal depression combined with a poor home environment is more likely to have an effect on cognitive development. However, the authors conclude that it may be that short term depression has no effect, where as long term depression could cause more serious problems. A further longitudinal study spanning 7 years again indicate no effect of maternal depression on cognitive development as a whole, however it found a gender difference in that boys are more susceptible to cognitive developmental issues when their mothers suffer depression.<sup id="cite_ref-Kurstjens_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurstjens-74">[74]</a></sup> This thread is continued in a study of children up to 2 years old.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornish_2005_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornish_2005-77">[77]</a></sup> The study reveals a significant difference on cognitive development between genders, with girls having a higher score, however this pattern is found regardless of the child’s mother’s history of depression. Infants with chronically depressed mothers showed significantly lower scores on the motor and mental scales within the Bayley Scales of Infant Development,<sup id="cite_ref-Cornish_2005_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornish_2005-77">[77]</a></sup> contrasting with many older studies.<sup id="cite_ref-Kurstjens_74-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurstjens-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Piteo_2012_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Piteo_2012-76">[76]</a></sup> A similar effect has been found at 11 years: male children of depressed mothers score an average of 19.4 points lower on an <a href="/wiki/Intelligence_quotient" title="Intelligence quotient">Intelligence Quotient IQ</a> test than those with healthy mothers, although this difference is much lower in girls.<sup id="cite_ref-Hay_2001_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hay_2001-78">[78]</a></sup> 3 month olds with depressed mothers show significantly lower scores on the Griffiths Mental Development Scale, which covers a range of developmental areas including cognitive, motor and social development.<sup id="cite_ref-Galler_2000_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galler_2000-79">[79]</a></sup> It has been suggested that interactions between depressed mothers and their children may affect social and cognitive abilities in later life.<sup id="cite_ref-Field_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Field-80">[80]</a></sup> Maternal depression has been shown to influence the mothers’ interaction with her child.<sup id="cite_ref-Reck_2004_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reck_2004-81">[81]</a></sup> When communicating with their child, depressed mothers fail to make changes to their vocal behaviour, and tend use unstructured vocal behaviours.<sup id="cite_ref-Bettes_1988_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bettes_1988-82">[82]</a></sup> Furthermore, when infants interact with depressed mothers they show signs of stress, such as increased pulse and raised cortisol levels, and make more use of avoidance behaviours, for example looking away, compared to those interacting with healthy mothers.<sup id="cite_ref-Field_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Field-80">[80]</a></sup> The effect of mother-infant interaction at 2 months has been shown to affect the child’s cognitive performance at 5 years.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Recent studies have begun to identify that other forms of psychopathology that may or may not be co-morbidly occurring with maternal depression can independently influence infants' and toddlers' subsequent social-emotional development through effects on regulatory processes within the child-parent attachment.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Maternal interpersonal violence-related <a href="/wiki/Post-traumatic_stress_disorder" class="mw-redirect" title="Post-traumatic stress disorder">post-traumatic stress disorder</a> (PTSD), for example, has been associated with subsequent dysregulation of emotion and aggression by ages 4–7 years.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maternal_drug_abuse">Maternal drug abuse</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Maternal drug abuse">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cocaine">Cocaine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Cocaine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prenatal_cocaine_exposure" title="Prenatal cocaine exposure">Prenatal cocaine exposure</a></div>
<p>Research has provided conflicting evidence regarding the effect of maternal substance abuse during and after pregnancy on children’s development.<sup id="cite_ref-Frank_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frank-75">[75]</a></sup> Children exposed to cocaine weigh less than those not exposed at numerous ages ranging from 6 to 30 months.<sup id="cite_ref-Hurt_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hurt-86">[86]</a></sup> Furthermore, studies indicate that the head circumference of children exposed to cocaine is lower than those unexposed.<sup id="cite_ref-Hurt_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hurt-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> On the other hand, two more recent studies found no significant differences between those exposed to cocaine and those who were not in either measure.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kilbride_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kilbride-89">[89]</a></sup> Maternal cocaine use may also affect the child’s cognitive development, with exposed children achieving lower scores on measures of psychomotor and mental development.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> However, again there is conflicting evidence, and a number of studies indicate no effect of maternal cocaine use on their child’s cognitive development.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Motor development can be imapired by maternal cocaine abuse.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> As is the case for cognitive and physical development, there are also studies showing no effect of cocaine use on motor development.<sup id="cite_ref-Hurt_86-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hurt-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kilbride_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kilbride-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_2">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The use of cocaine by pregnant women is not the only drug that can have a negative effect on the fetus. Tobacco, marijuana, and opiates are also the types of drugs that can effect an unborn child’s cognitive and behavioral development. Smoking tobacco increases pregnancy complications including low birth rate, prematurity, placental abruption, and intrauterine death. It can also cause disturbed maternal-infant interaction; reduced IQ, ADHD, and it can especially cause tobacco use in the child. Parental marijuana exposure may have long-term emotional and behavioral consequences. A ten-year-old child who had been exposed to the drug during pregnancy reported more depressive symptoms than fetuses unexposed. Some short-term effects include executive function impairment, reading difficulty, and delayed state regulation. An opiate drug, such as heroin, decreases birth weight, birth length, and head circumference when exposed to the fetus. Parental opiate exposure has greater conflicting impact than parental cocaine exposure on the infant’s Central Nervous System and autonomic nervous system. There are also some negative consequences on a child that you wouldn’t think of with opiates, such as: less rhythmic swallowing, strabismus, and feelings of rejection.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Malnutrition">Malnutrition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Malnutrition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Poor nutrition early in life contributes to <a href="/wiki/Stunted_growth" title="Stunted growth">stunting</a>, and by the age of two or three can be associated with cognitive deficits, poor school achievement, and poor social relationships later in life <sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">Malnutrition</a> is a large problem in developing nations, and has an important effect on young children’s weight and height.Children suffering malnutrition in Colombia weighed less than those living in upper class conditions at the age of 36 months (11.88&#160;kg compared to 14&#160;kg), similarly, malnourished children were shorter than well-nourished children, again at 36 months (85.3&#160;cm in malnourished children; 94&#160;cm in well-nourished children <sup id="cite_ref-Mora_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mora-73">[73]</a></sup> Malnutrition has been indicated as a negative influence on childhood <a href="/wiki/Intelligence_quotient" title="Intelligence quotient">Intelligence Quotient IQ</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pmid12796242_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12796242-99">[99]</a></sup> Although it is now suggested that this effect is nullified when parental IQ is considered, implying that this difference is genetic.<sup id="cite_ref-Webb_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webb-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrients">Nutrients</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Nutrients">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The effect of low iron levels on cognitive development and IQ is a subject still to reach consensus.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Some evidence suggests that well-nourished children with lower levels of iron and folate (although not at such a level to be considered deficient) have a lower IQ than those with higher levels of iron and folate.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Furthermore, anaemic children perform worse on cognitive measures than non-anaemic children.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> These nutrients have been strongly implicated in brain development, along with iodine and zinc.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Iodine is required for the generation of thyroid hormones necessary for brain development.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Iodine deficiency may reduce IQ by an average of 13.5 points compared to healthy individual.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Zinc deficiency has also been shown to slow childhood growth and development.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Socioeconomic_status">Socioeconomic status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Socioeconomic status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Socioeconomic status is measured primarily based on the factors of income, educational attainment and occupation.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Current investigations into the role of socioeconomic factors on child development repeatedly show that continual poverty is more harmful on <a href="/wiki/Intelligence_quotient" title="Intelligence quotient">Intelligence Quotient IQ</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-110">[110]</a></sup> and cognitive abilities<sup id="cite_ref-Schoon_2011_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schoon_2011-111">[111]</a></sup> than short-lived poverty. Children in families who experience persistent financial hardships and poverty have significantly impaired cognitive abilities compared to those in families who do not face this issue.<sup id="cite_ref-Schoon_2011_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schoon_2011-111">[111]</a></sup> Low income poverty can cause a number of further issues shown to effect child development, such as poor academic success, less family involvement, iron deficiency, infections, a lack of stimulation,<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> malnutrition and lead poisoning due to lead paint found on the walls of some houses.<sup id="cite_ref-Brooks-Gunn_1997_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brooks-Gunn_1997-113">[113]</a></sup> Child blood levels of lead increase as income decreases.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Income poverty is associated with a 6–13 point reduction in IQ for those earning half of the poverty threshold compared to those earning twice the poverty threshold.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-110">[110]</a></sup> That being said, children coming from households featuring continual or temporary poverty still perform lower than children in middle-class families.<sup id="cite_ref-Schoon_2011_111-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schoon_2011-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>Parental educational attainment is the most significant socioeconomic factor in predicting the child’s cognitive abilities,<sup id="cite_ref-DeGarmo_2003_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeGarmo_2003-115">[115]</a></sup> those with a mother with high IQ are likely to have higher IQs themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-Webb_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webb-100">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Similarly, maternal occupation is associated with better cognitive achievement. Those whose mothers’ job entails problem-solving are more likely to be given stimulating tasks and games, and are likely to achieve more advanced verbal competency.<sup id="cite_ref-Parcel_1990_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parcel_1990-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>Low-income families are less likely to provide a stimulating home learning environment to their children due to time constraints and financial stress.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Compared to two-parent households, children with a single-parent generally don't have better social, behavioral, educational, or cognitive outcomes than those with two parents because of economic vulnerability and a lack of parental involvement.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> A child's academic achievement is influenced by parents' educational attainment, parenting style, and parental investment in their child's cognitive and educational success. Upper-income families are able to afford learning opportunities inside and outside of the classroom.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Poverty-stricken children are subjected to fewer stimulating recreational activities, often missing out on trips to libraries or museums, and are unable to access a tutor to help with problematic academic areas.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>A further factor in a child’s educational attainment involves the school environment, more specifically teacher expectations and attitudes.<sup id="cite_ref-McLoyd_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLoyd-122">[122]</a></sup> It has been argued that teachers perceive low-SES children as being less academically able and as such provide them with less attention and reinforcement.<sup id="cite_ref-McLoyd_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLoyd-122">[122]</a></sup> On the other hand, it has been found that when schools make an effort to increases family and school involvement, children perform better on state tests.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parasites">Parasites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Parasites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Diarrhea caused by the parasitic disease Giardiasis is associated with lower IQ.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Parasitic worms (helminths) are associated with nutritional deficiencies that are known to be a risk to child development.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> In particular, intestinal parasitism being one of the most neglected tropical diseases in the developed world. Harboring of this parasite could adverse several health implications in children affecting childhood development and morbidity. Reducing the prevalence of the parasite can be a benefit in child growth, development, and educational outcome.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poisoning">Poisoning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Poisoning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>High levels of lead in the blood is associated with attention deficits,<sup id="cite_ref-Calderon_2001_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calderon_2001-127">[127]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Arsenic_poisoning" title="Arsenic poisoning">arsenic poisoning</a> has a negative effect on verbal and full <a href="/wiki/Intelligence_quotient" title="Intelligence quotient">Intelligence Quotient IQ</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Calderon_2001_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calderon_2001-127">[127]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Manganism" title="Manganism">Manganese poisoning</a> due to levels in drinking water is also associated with a reduced IQ of 6.2 points between the highest and lowest level of poisoning.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Prenatal exposure to various <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> including organophosphates,<sup id="cite_ref-Bouchard_2011_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bouchard_2011-129">[129]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Chlorpyrifos" title="Chlorpyrifos">chlorpyrifos</a><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> has also been linked to reduced IQ score. <a href="/wiki/Organophosphate" title="Organophosphate">Organophosphates</a> have been specifically linked to poorer <a href="/wiki/Working_memory" title="Working memory">working memory</a>, verbal comprehension, perceptual reasoning and processing speed.<sup id="cite_ref-Bouchard_2011_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bouchard_2011-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Obesity">Obesity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Obesity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A major problem in childhood is obesity. In America, the number of obese children is rapidly increasing. <a href="/wiki/Childhood_obesity" title="Childhood obesity">Childhood obesity</a> is caused by a variety of factors. The main causes of childhood obesity are "lifestyle issues-too little activity and too many calories."<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Child obesity can cause major problems with a child’s development. In order for a child to develop successfully, he or she must grow up in a positive environment with good health and academics. If a child becomes obese, there will be consequences such as: depression, low self-esteem, eating disorders, medical problems, and so on. These issues of childhood obesity can be slowed, if society focuses on the causes. If parents enforce a healthy lifestyle at home with physical activity and proper dieting, many issues with obesity could be avoided. Focusing on the causes may, over time, decrease the consequences of childhood obesity and therefore strengthen a child’s development to grow.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_3">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Cognitive development is related to childhood exposure to violence and trauma, including spousal abuse between the parents and sexual abuse.<sup id="cite_ref-Bosquet_Enlow_2012_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bosquet_Enlow_2012-133">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Intrauterine growth retardation is associated with learning deficits in childhood, and as such, is related to lower IQ.<sup id="cite_ref-Low_1992_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Low_1992-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Neglect">Neglect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: Neglect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>When a child is unable to meet their developmental goals, because they have not been provided with the correct amount of care, stimulation or nutrition this situation is commonly referred to as <a href="/wiki/Child_neglect" title="Child neglect">child neglect</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assessing_and_identifying">Assessing and identifying</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: Assessing and identifying">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Assessing and identifying neglect pose a number of challenges for practitioners. Given that neglect is a dynamic between the child's development and levels of nurturance, the question in identifying neglect, becomes one of where do you start, with the child's development or with the levels of nurturance?</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_focused_methods">Development focused methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=61" title="Edit section: Development focused methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some professionals identify neglect by measuring the developmental levels of a child, for if those developmental levels are normal, one can, by definition, conclude that a child is not being neglected. Areas of development that could be measured include weight, height, stamina, social &amp; emotional responses, speech and motor development. Interestingly all these features go up to make a medical assessment of whether a child is thriving, so that a professional looking to start an assessment of neglect, might reasonably start with information collected by a doctor. Infants are often weighed and measured when seen by their physicians for <a href="/w/index.php?title=Well-baby_visit&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Well-baby visit (page does not exist)">well-baby check-ups</a>. The physician initiates a more complete evaluation when the infant's development and functioning are found to be delayed. What this suggests is that social work staff could consult medical notes to establish if the baby or child is failing to thrive, as a first step in a pathway towards identifying neglect. If developmental levels are subnormal, then the identification of neglect then requires the professional establish if those subnormal levels of development can be put down to the level of nurturance experienced by the child. One needs to discount that the developmental delay was caused by some genetic condition or disease, which do not have their basis in a lack of nurturance.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Starting_the_assessment">Starting the assessment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=62" title="Edit section: Starting the assessment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Another way of starting a process for identifying neglect is to identify if the child in question is experiencing a level of nurturance lower than that considered necessary to support normal development.<sup id="cite_ref-Daniel.2C_B._20112_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daniel.2C_B._20112-136">[136]</a></sup> In part this requires a knowledge of the level of nurturance required by the child to sustain normal development, which might be particular to his or her age, gender and other factors.<sup id="cite_ref-Daniel.2C_B._20112_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daniel.2C_B._20112-136">[136]</a></sup> However quite how one ascertains what a particular child needs, without referring back to their level of development, is not something theory and policy on neglect is clear about. Furthermore, ascertaining whether a child is getting the requisite level of nurturance needs to take into account not just the intensity of the nurturance, but also, given that the intensity of certain forms of nurturance can cary across time, the duration and frequency of the nurturance. It is acceptable for a child to experience varying and low levels of certain types of nurturance across a day and from time to time, however, the levels of nurturance should never cross thresholds of intensity, duration and frequency. For this reason professionals are minded to keep detailed histories of care provision, which demonstrate the duration to which the child is exposed to periods of subnormal exposure to care, stimulation and nutrition.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Starting_the_assessment_2">Starting the assessment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=63" title="Edit section: Starting the assessment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>It is most common for guidance to suggest professionals should focus on the levels of nurturance provided by the carers of the child, where neglect is understood as an issue of the parents' behaviour towards the child.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Some authors feel that establishing the failure of parents and caregivers to provide care would be sufficient to conclude that neglect was occurring.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Action for Children <sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> state that, "A child experiences neglect when the adults who look after them fail to meet their needs" clearly defining neglect as a matter of parental performance. This raises the question about what level of nurturance, a carer or parent needs to fall under, to provoke developmental delay, and how one goes about measuring that accurately.</p>
<p>The method, which focuses on the stimulation provided by the carer, can be subject to critique. Neglect is about the child’s development being adversely affected by the levels of nurturance, but the carers' provision of nurturance is not always a good indicator of the level of nurturance received by the child. Neglect may be occurring at school, outside of parental care. The child may be receiving nurturance from siblings or through a boarding school education, which compensates for the lack of nurturance provided by the parents.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Linking_to_stimulation">Linking to stimulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=64" title="Edit section: Linking to stimulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Neglect is a process whereby children experience developmental delay owing to experiencing insufficient levels of nurturance. It has been argued that in principle, this means that when starting an assessment of neglect by identifying developmental delay one needs to then check the levels of nurturance received by the child. Certainly, where guidance on identifying neglect does urge for practitioners to measure developmental levels, some guidance urges practitioners to focus on how developmental levels can be attributed to parental behaviour.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> However the narrow focus on parental behaviour can be criticised for unnecessarily ruling out the possible effect of institutionalised neglect, e.g. neglect at school.</p>
<p>If one starts by concluding that the levels of nurture received by the child are insufficient, one then needs to consider the developmental levels achieved by the child.</p>
<p>Further challenges arise however. Even when one has established developmental delay and exposure to low levels of nurture, one needs to rule out the possibility that the link between the two is coincidental. The developmental delay may be caused by a genetic disorder, disease or physical, sexual or emotional abuse. Of course the developmental delay may be caused by a mixture of underexposure to nurture, abuse, genetics and disease.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Practical_tools_for_measuring">Practical tools for measuring</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=65" title="Edit section: Practical tools for measuring">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/The_Graded_Care_Profile_Tool" title="The Graded Care Profile Tool">The Graded Care Profile Tool</a> is a practice tool which gives an objective measure of the quality of care in terms of a parent/carer's commitment. It was developed in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_Family_Assessment_Scale" title="North Carolina Family Assessment Scale">North Carolina Family Assessment Scale</a> is a tool which can be used by a practitioner to explore whether neglect is taking place across a range of family functioning areas.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intervention_programs_for_addressing">Intervention programs for addressing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=66" title="Edit section: Intervention programs for addressing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Early intervention programs and treatments in developed countries include individual counselling, family, group counselling and social support services, behavioural skills training programs to eliminate problematic behaviour and teach parents "appropriate" parenting behaviour.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Parenting_programs">Parenting programs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=67" title="Edit section: Parenting programs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Video_interaction_guidance" title="Video interaction guidance">Video interaction guidance</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Video_feedback_intervention" title="Video feedback intervention">video feedback intervention</a> through which a "guider" helps a client to enhance communication within relationships. The client is guided to analyse and reflect on video clips of their own interactions.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> Video Interaction Guidance has been used where concerns have been expressed over possible parental neglect in cases where the focus child is aged 2–12, and where the child is not the subject of a child protection plan.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/The_SafeCare_programme" class="mw-redirect" title="The SafeCare programme">The SafeCare programme</a> is a preventative programme working with parents of children under 6 years old who are at risk of experiencing significant harm through neglect. The programme is delivered in the home by trained practitioners, over 18 to 20 sessions and focuses on 3 key areas: parent-infant/child interaction; home safety and child health.<sup id="cite_ref-nspcc.org.uk2_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nspcc.org.uk2-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Triple_P_(Parenting_Program)" class="mw-redirect" title="Triple P (Parenting Program)">Triple P (Parenting Program)</a> is a positive parenting program. It is a multilevel, parenting and family support strategy. The idea behind it is that if parents are educated on "proper" parenting and given the appropriate resources, it could help decrease the amount of child neglect cases. When deciding whether to leave a child home alone, caregivers need to consider the child's physical, mental, and emotional well-being, as well as state laws and policies regarding this issue.<sup id="cite_ref-nichd.nih.gov_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nichd.nih.gov-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Motivation_and_inspiration">Motivation and inspiration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Child_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=68" title="Edit section: Motivation and inspiration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A good parenting skill will involve motivating and inspiring children during their upbringing to help them to survive and succeed in life. It is more vital to emphasize on intrinsic motivation than on extrinsic motivation in children.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
